          <div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">

       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:General_relativity_sidebar" title="Template:General relativity sidebar">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:General_relativity_sidebar" title="Template talk:General relativity sidebar">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:General_relativity_sidebar&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Tests of general relativity
       </b>
       serve to establish observational evidence for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_general_relativity" title="Theory of general relativity">
        theory of general relativity
       </a>
       . The first three tests, proposed by Einstein in 1915, concerned the "anomalous"
       <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">
        precession
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">
        perihelion
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
        Mercury
       </a>
       , the bending of light in
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">
        gravitational fields
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_redshift" title="Gravitational redshift">
        gravitational redshift
       </a>
       . The precession of Mercury was already known; experiments showing light bending in line with the predictions of general relativity was found in 1919, with increasing precision measurements done in subsequent tests, and astrophysical measurement of the gravitational redshift was claimed to be measured in 1925, although measurements sensitive enough to actually confirm the theory were not done until 1954. A program of more accurate tests starting in 1959 tested the various predictions of general relativity with a further degree of accuracy in the weak gravitational field limit, severely limiting possible deviations from the theory.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the 1970s, additional tests began to be made, starting with Irwin Shapiro's measurement of the relativistic time delay in radar signal travel time near the sun. Beginning in 1974,
       <a href="/wiki/Russell_Alan_Hulse" title="Russell Alan Hulse">
        Hulse
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Hooton_Taylor,_Jr." title="Joseph Hooton Taylor, Jr.">
        Taylor
       </a>
       and others have studied the behaviour of
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_pulsar" title="Binary pulsar">
        binary pulsars
       </a>
       experiencing much stronger gravitational fields than those found in the Solar System. Both in the weak field limit (as in the Solar System) and with the stronger fields present in systems of binary pulsars the predictions of general relativity have been extremely well tested locally.
      </p>
      <p>
       In February 2016, the
       <a href="/wiki/LIGO" title="LIGO">
        Advanced LIGO
       </a>
       team announced that they had
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave_detection" title="Gravitational wave detection">
        directly detected gravitational waves
       </a>
       from a black hole merger.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery_2016_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Discovery_2016-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This discovery, along with additional detections announced in June 2016 and June 2017,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conover2017_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-conover2017-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       tested general relativity in the very strong field limit, observing to date no deviations from theory.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_tests">
        Classical tests
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classical tests">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">
        Albert Einstein
       </a>
       proposed
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ein1916_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ein1916-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ein1916eng_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ein1916eng-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       three tests of general relativity, subsequently called
       <b>
        the classical tests of general relativity
       </b>
       , in 1916:
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        the perihelion precession of
        <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
         Mercury
        </a>
        's orbit
       </li>
       <li>
        the
        <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">
         deflection of light
        </a>
        by the
        <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
         Sun
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        the
        <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_redshift" title="Gravitational redshift">
         gravitational redshift
        </a>
        of light
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       In the letter to the
       <a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">
        London
        <i>
         Times
        </i>
       </a>
       on November 28, 1919, he described the theory of relativity and thanked his English colleagues for their understanding and testing of his work. He also mentioned three classical tests with comments:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        "The chief attraction of the theory lies in its logical completeness. If a single one of the conclusions drawn from it proves wrong, it must be given up; to modify it without destroying the whole structure seems to be impossible."
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Perihelion_precession_of_Mercury">
        Perihelion precession of Mercury
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Perihelion precession of Mercury">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_transit_2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2000" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Mercury_transit_2.jpg/150px-Mercury_transit_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Mercury_transit_2.jpg/225px-Mercury_transit_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Mercury_transit_2.jpg/300px-Mercury_transit_2.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_transit_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Transit of Mercury on November 8, 2006 with
         <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">
          sunspots
         </a>
         #921, 922, and 923
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apsidendrehung.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="1900" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Apsidendrehung.png/150px-Apsidendrehung.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Apsidendrehung.png/225px-Apsidendrehung.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Apsidendrehung.png/300px-Apsidendrehung.png 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apsidendrehung.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The perihelion precession of Mercury
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For more details on this topic, see
       <a href="/wiki/Two-body_problem_in_general_relativity" title="Two-body problem in general relativity">
        Two-body problem in general relativity
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       Under Newtonian physics, a two-body system consisting of a lone object orbiting a spherical mass would trace out an
       <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">
        ellipse
       </a>
       with the spherical mass at a
       <a href="/wiki/Focus_(geometry)" title="Focus (geometry)">
        focus
       </a>
       . The point of closest approach, called the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periapsis" title="Periapsis">
        periapsis
       </a>
       (or, because the central body in the Solar System is the Sun,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">
        perihelion
       </a>
       ), is fixed. A number of effects in the Solar System cause the perihelia of planets to precess (rotate) around the Sun. The principal cause is the presence of other planets which
       <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">
        perturb
       </a>
       one another's orbit. Another (much less significant) effect is solar
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">
        oblateness
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Mercury deviates from the precession predicted from these Newtonian effects. This anomalous rate of precession of the perihelion of Mercury's orbit was first recognized in 1859 as a problem in
       <a href="/wiki/Celestial_mechanics" title="Celestial mechanics">
        celestial mechanics
       </a>
       , by
       <a href="/wiki/Urbain_Le_Verrier" title="Urbain Le Verrier">
        Urbain Le Verrier
       </a>
       . His reanalysis of available timed observations of transits of
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
        Mercury
       </a>
       over the Sun's disk from 1697 to 1848 showed that the actual rate of the precession disagreed from that predicted from Newton's theory by 38″ (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arc_seconds" title="Arc seconds">
        arc seconds
       </a>
       ) per
       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_year" title="Tropical year">
        tropical century
       </a>
       (later re-estimated at 43″ by
       <a href="/wiki/Simon_Newcomb" title="Simon Newcomb">
        Simon Newcomb
       </a>
       in 1882).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A number of
       <i>
        ad hoc
       </i>
       and ultimately unsuccessful solutions were proposed, but they tended to introduce more problems.
      </p>
      <p>
       In general relativity, this remaining
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_precession" title="Stellar precession">
        precession
       </a>
       , or change of orientation of the orbital ellipse within its orbital plane, is explained by gravitation being mediated by the curvature of spacetime. Einstein showed that general relativity
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ein1916_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ein1916-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       agrees closely with the observed amount of perihelion shift. This was a powerful factor motivating the adoption of general relativity. Recently, Friedman and Steiner
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merc_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Merc-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       obtained the observed perihelion shift and explained its origin without curving spacetime using the
       <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_Newtonian_dynamics" title="Relativistic Newtonian dynamics">
        relativistic Newtonian dynamics
       </a>
       model.
      </p>
      <p>
       Although earlier measurements of planetary orbits were made using conventional telescopes, more accurate measurements are now made with
       <a href="/wiki/Radar_astronomy" title="Radar astronomy">
        radar
       </a>
       . The total observed precession of Mercury is 574.10″±0.65 per century
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clemence_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Clemence-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       relative to the inertial
       <a href="/wiki/International_Celestial_Reference_Frame" title="International Celestial Reference Frame">
        ICRF
       </a>
       . This precession can be attributed to the following causes:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
       <caption>
        <b>
         Sources of the precession of perihelion for Mercury
        </b>
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Amount (arcsec/Julian century)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
          <a href="#cite_note-9">
           [9]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
        <th>
         Cause
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         532.3035
        </td>
        <td>
         Gravitational tugs of other solar bodies
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         0.0286
        </td>
        <td>
         Oblateness of the Sun (
         <a href="/wiki/Quadrupole#Gravitational_quadrupole" title="Quadrupole">
          quadrupole moment
         </a>
         )
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         42.9799
        </td>
        <td>
         Gravitoelectric effects (Schwarzschild-like)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         −0.0020
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Lense%E2%80%93Thirring_precession" title="Lense–Thirring precession">
          Lense–Thirring precession
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         575.31
        </td>
        <td>
         Total predicted
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         574.10±0.65
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clemence_8-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-Clemence-8">
           [8]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
        <td>
         Observed
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The correction by 42.98″ is 3/2 multiple of classical prediction with
       <a href="/wiki/Parameterized_post-Newtonian_formalism" title="Parameterized post-Newtonian formalism">
        PPN parameters
       </a>
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \gamma =\beta =1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              γ
              <!-- γ -->
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mi>
              β
              <!-- β -->
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              1
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \gamma =\beta =1}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\displaystyle \gamma =\beta =1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fb96b68c1387c6ca1938c729d89302b6cdd5ceb4" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:9.954ex; height:2.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus the effect can be fully explained by general relativity. More recent calculations based on more precise measurements have not materially changed the situation.
      </p>
      <p>
       In general relativity the perihelion shift
       <i>
        σ
       </i>
       , expressed in radians per revolution, is approximately given by:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \sigma ={\frac {24\pi ^{3}L^{2}}{T^{2}c^{2}(1-e^{2})}}\ ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               σ
               <!-- σ -->
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mn>
                  24
                 </mn>
                 <msup>
                  <mi>
                   π
                   <!-- π -->
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    3
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msup>
                 <msup>
                  <mi>
                   L
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    2
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msup>
                </mrow>
                <mrow>
                 <msup>
                  <mi>
                   T
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    2
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msup>
                 <msup>
                  <mi>
                   c
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    2
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msup>
                 <mo stretchy="false">
                  (
                 </mo>
                 <mn>
                  1
                 </mn>
                 <mo>
                  −
                  <!-- − -->
                 </mo>
                 <msup>
                  <mi>
                   e
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    2
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msup>
                 <mo stretchy="false">
                  )
                 </mo>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mtext>
              </mtext>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \sigma ={\frac {24\pi ^{3}L^{2}}{T^{2}c^{2}(1-e^{2})}}\ ,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle \sigma ={\frac {24\pi ^{3}L^{2}}{T^{2}c^{2}(1-e^{2})}}\ ,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e58b27c973a8dcd18661d17dbd71493077b077ce" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:19.277ex; height:6.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <i>
        L
       </i>
       is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">
        semi-major axis
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">
        orbital period
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       is the speed of light, and
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">
        orbital eccentricity
       </a>
       (see:
       <a href="/wiki/Two-body_problem_in_general_relativity" title="Two-body problem in general relativity">
        Two-body problem in general relativity
       </a>
       ). This formula gives the exact expression for the perihelion shift derived in
       <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_Newtonian_dynamics" title="Relativistic Newtonian dynamics">
        relativistic Newtonian dynamics
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bin_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bin-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The other planets experience perihelion shifts as well, but, since they are farther from the Sun and have longer periods, their shifts are lower, and could not be observed accurately until long after Mercury's. For example, the perihelion shift of Earth's orbit due to general relativity is of 3.84″ per century, and Venus's is 8.62″. Both values have now been measured, with results in good agreement with theory.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biswas_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Biswas-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periapsis" title="Periapsis">
        periapsis
       </a>
       shift has also now been measured for binary pulsar systems, with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PSR_1913%2B16" title="PSR 1913+16">
        PSR 1913+16
       </a>
       amounting to 4.2º per year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matzner_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Matzner-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These observations are consistent with general relativity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is also possible to measure periapsis shift in binary star systems which do not contain ultra-dense stars, but it is more difficult to model the classical effects precisely – for example, the alignment of the stars' spin to their orbital plane needs to be known and is hard to measure directly. A few systems, such as
       <a href="/wiki/DI_Herculis" title="DI Herculis">
        DI Herculis
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skyandtelescope2009_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-skyandtelescope2009-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       have been measured as test cases for general relativity.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Deflection_of_light_by_the_Sun">
        Deflection of light by the Sun
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Deflection of light by the Sun">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1919_eclipse_negative.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="899" data-file-width="700" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1919_eclipse_negative.jpg/150px-1919_eclipse_negative.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1919_eclipse_negative.jpg/225px-1919_eclipse_negative.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1919_eclipse_negative.jpg/300px-1919_eclipse_negative.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1919_eclipse_negative.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         One of
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Arthur_Eddington" title="Sir Arthur Eddington">
          Eddington
         </a>
         's photographs of the 1919
         <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">
          solar eclipse
         </a>
         experiment, presented in his 1920 paper announcing its success
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">
        Henry Cavendish
       </a>
       in 1784 (in an unpublished manuscript) and
       <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_von_Soldner" title="Johann Georg von Soldner">
        Johann Georg von Soldner
       </a>
       in 1801 (published in 1804) had pointed out that Newtonian gravity predicts that starlight will bend around a massive object.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The same value as Soldner's was calculated by Einstein in 1911 based on the equivalence principle alone. However, Einstein noted in 1915 in the process of completing general relativity, that his (and thus Soldner's) 1911 result is only half of the correct value. Einstein became the first to calculate the correct value for light bending.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first observation of light deflection was performed by noting the change in position of
       <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
        stars
       </a>
       as they passed near the Sun on the
       <a href="/wiki/Celestial_sphere" title="Celestial sphere">
        celestial sphere
       </a>
       . The observations were performed by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Arthur_Eddington" title="Sir Arthur Eddington">
        Arthur Eddington
       </a>
       and his collaborators during the total
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_May_29,_1919" title="Solar eclipse of May 29, 1919">
        solar eclipse of May 29, 1919
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eddington1920_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Eddington1920-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       when the stars near the Sun (at that time in the constellation
       <a href="/wiki/Taurus_(constellation)" title="Taurus (constellation)">
        Taurus
       </a>
       ) could be observed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eddington1920_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Eddington1920-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Observations were made simultaneously in the cities of
       <a href="/wiki/Sobral,_Cear%C3%A1" title="Sobral, Ceará">
        Sobral, Ceará
       </a>
       , Brazil and in
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_and_Pr%C3%ADncipe" title="São Tomé and Príncipe">
        São Tomé and Príncipe
       </a>
       on the west coast of Africa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The result was considered spectacular news and made the front page of most major newspapers. It made Einstein and his theory of general relativity world-famous. When asked by his assistant what his reaction would have been if general relativity had not been confirmed by Eddington and Dyson in 1919, Einstein famously made the quip: "Then I would feel sorry for the dear Lord. The theory is correct anyway."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The early accuracy, however, was poor. The results were argued by some
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to have been plagued by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systematic_error" title="Systematic error">
        systematic error
       </a>
       and possibly
       <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_bias" title="Confirmation bias">
        confirmation bias
       </a>
       , although modern reanalysis of the dataset
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       suggests that Eddington's analysis was accurate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysToday_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PhysToday-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The measurement was repeated by a team from the
       <a href="/wiki/Lick_Observatory" title="Lick Observatory">
        Lick Observatory
       </a>
       in the 1922 eclipse, with results that agreed with the 1919 results
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysToday_26-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-PhysToday-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and has been repeated several times since, most notably in 1953 by
       <a href="/wiki/Yerkes_Observatory" title="Yerkes Observatory">
        Yerkes Observatory
       </a>
       astronomers
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and in 1973 by a team from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Texas" title="University of Texas">
        University of Texas
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Considerable uncertainty remained in these measurements for almost fifty years, until observations started being made at
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">
        radio frequencies
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the Sun is too close by for an
       <a href="/wiki/Einstein_ring" title="Einstein ring">
        Einstein ring
       </a>
       to lie outside its corona, such a ring formed by the deflection of light from distant galaxies has been observed for a nearby star.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_redshift_of_light">
        Gravitational redshift of light
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Gravitational redshift of light">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_redshift#Experimental_Verification" title="Gravitational redshift">
        Gravitational redshift § Experimental Verification
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gravitational_red-shifting2.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Gravitational_red-shifting2.png/150px-Gravitational_red-shifting2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Gravitational_red-shifting2.png/225px-Gravitational_red-shifting2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Gravitational_red-shifting2.png/300px-Gravitational_red-shifting2.png 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gravitational_red-shifting2.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The gravitational redshift of a light wave as it moves upwards against a gravitational field (caused by the yellow star below).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Einstein predicted the
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_redshift" title="Gravitational redshift">
        gravitational redshift
       </a>
       of light from the
       <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">
        equivalence principle
       </a>
       in 1907, and it was predicted that this effect might be measured in the spectral lines of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_dwarf_star" title="White dwarf star">
        white dwarf star
       </a>
       , which has a very high gravitational field. Initial attempts to measure the gravitational redshift of the spectrum of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sirius-B" title="Sirius-B">
        Sirius-B
       </a>
       , were done by
       <a href="/wiki/Walter_Sydney_Adams" title="Walter Sydney Adams">
        Walter Sydney Adams
       </a>
       in 1925, but the result was criticized as being unusable due to the contamination from light from the (much brighter) primary star,
       <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">
        Sirius
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hetherington1980_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hetherington1980-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holberg2010_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holberg2010-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first accurate measurement of the gravitational redshift of a white dwarf was done by Popper in 1954, measuring a 21 km/sec gravitational redshift of
       <a href="/wiki/40_Eridani" title="40 Eridani">
        40 Eridani
       </a>
       B.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holberg2010_32-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Holberg2010-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The redshift of Sirius B was finally measured by Greenstein
       <i>
        et al.
       </i>
       in 1971, obtaining the value for the gravitational redshift of 89±19 km/sec, with more accurate measurements by the Hubble Space Telescope showing 80.4±4.8 km/sec.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tests_of_Special_Relativity">
        Tests of Special Relativity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Tests of Special Relativity">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The general theory of relativity incorporates Einstein's
       <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">
        special theory of relativity
       </a>
       , and hence test of special relativity are also testing aspects of general relativity. As a consequence of the
       <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">
        equivalence principle
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorentz_invariance" title="Lorentz invariance">
        Lorentz invariance
       </a>
       holds locally in non-rotating, freely falling reference frames. Experiments related to Lorentz invariance special relativity (that is, when gravitational effects can be neglected) are described in
       <a href="/wiki/Tests_of_special_relativity" title="Tests of special relativity">
        Tests of special relativity
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_tests">
        Modern tests
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern tests">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The modern era of testing general relativity was ushered in largely at the impetus of
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Dicke" title="Robert H. Dicke">
        Dicke
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leonard_Isaac_Schiff" title="Leonard Isaac Schiff">
        Schiff
       </a>
       who laid out a framework for testing general relativity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They emphasized the importance not only of the classical tests, but of null experiments, testing for effects which in principle could occur in a theory of gravitation, but do not occur in general relativity. Other important theoretical developments included the inception of
       <a href="/wiki/Alternatives_to_general_relativity" title="Alternatives to general relativity">
        alternative theories to general relativity
       </a>
       , in particular,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scalar-tensor_theory" title="Scalar-tensor theory">
        scalar-tensor theories
       </a>
       such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Brans%E2%80%93Dicke_theory" title="Brans–Dicke theory">
        Brans–Dicke theory
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Parameterized_post-Newtonian_formalism" title="Parameterized post-Newtonian formalism">
        parameterized post-Newtonian formalism
       </a>
       in which deviations from general relativity can be quantified; and the framework of the
       <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">
        equivalence principle
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Experimentally, new developments in
       <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">
        space exploration
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">
        electronics
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Condensed_matter_physics" title="Condensed matter physics">
        condensed matter physics
       </a>
       have made additional precise experiments possible, such as the Pound–Rebka experiment, laser interferometry and lunar rangefinding.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Newtonian_tests_of_gravity">
        Post-Newtonian tests of gravity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-Newtonian tests of gravity">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Early tests of general relativity were hampered by the lack of viable competitors to the theory: it was not clear what sorts of tests would distinguish it from its competitors. General relativity was the only known relativistic theory of gravity compatible with special relativity and observations. Moreover, it is an extremely simple and elegant theory.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch">
          <span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2017)">
           according to whom?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       This changed with the introduction of
       <a href="/wiki/Brans%E2%80%93Dicke_theory" title="Brans–Dicke theory">
        Brans–Dicke theory
       </a>
       in 1960. This theory is arguably simpler, as it contains no
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dimensionless_number" title="Dimensionless number">
        dimensionful
       </a>
       constants, and is compatible with a version of
       <a href="/wiki/Mach%27s_principle" title="Mach's principle">
        Mach's principle
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dirac" title="Paul Dirac">
        Dirac's
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Dirac_large_numbers_hypothesis" title="Dirac large numbers hypothesis">
        large numbers hypothesis
       </a>
       , two philosophical ideas which have been influential in the history of relativity. Ultimately, this led to the development of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PPN_formalism" title="PPN formalism">
        parametrized post-Newtonian formalism
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Nordtvedt" title="Kenneth Nordtvedt">
        Nordtvedt
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Martin_Will" title="Clifford Martin Will">
        Will
       </a>
       , which parametrizes, in terms of ten adjustable parameters, all the possible departures from Newton's law of universal gravitation to first order in the velocity of moving objects (
       <i>
        i.e.
       </i>
       to first order in
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle v/c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              v
             </mi>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mo>
               /
              </mo>
             </mrow>
             <mi>
              c
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle v/c}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="v/c" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bd5fbfe05e197127354e233f6c813e6b5d846672" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.297ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       , where
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       is the velocity of an object and
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       is the speed of light). This approximation allows the possible deviations from general relativity, for slowly moving objects in weak gravitational fields, to be systematically analyzed. Much effort has been put into constraining the post-Newtonian parameters, and deviations from general relativity are at present severely limited.
      </p>
      <p>
       The experiments testing gravitational lensing and light time delay limits the same post-Newtonian parameter, the so-called Eddington parameter γ, which is a straightforward parametrization of the amount of deflection of light by a gravitational source. It is equal to one for general relativity, and takes different values in other theories (such as Brans–Dicke theory). It is the best constrained of the ten post-Newtonian parameters, but there are other experiments designed to constrain the others. Precise observations of the perihelion shift of Mercury constrain other parameters, as do tests of the strong equivalence principle.
      </p>
      <p>
       One of the goals of the mission
       <a href="/wiki/BepiColombo" title="BepiColombo">
        BepiColombo
       </a>
       is testing the general relativity theory by measuring the parameters gamma and beta of the parametrized post-Newtonian formalism with high accuracy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_lensing">
        Gravitational lensing
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Gravitational lensing">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       One of the most important tests is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" title="Gravitational lensing">
        gravitational lensing
       </a>
       . It has been observed in distant astrophysical sources, but these are poorly controlled and it is uncertain how they constrain general relativity. The most precise tests are analogous to Eddington's 1919 experiment: they measure the deflection of radiation from a distant source by the Sun. The sources that can be most precisely analyzed are distant
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">
        radio sources
       </a>
       . In particular, some
       <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">
        quasars
       </a>
       are very strong radio sources. The directional resolution of any telescope is in principle limited by diffraction; for radio telescopes this is also the practical limit. An important improvement in obtaining positional high accuracies (from milli-arcsecond to micro-arcsecond) was obtained by combining radio telescopes across Earth. The technique is called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VLBI" title="VLBI">
        very long baseline interferometry
       </a>
       (VLBI). With this technique radio observations couple the phase information of the radio signal observed in telescopes separated over large distances. Recently, these telescopes have measured the deflection of radio waves by the Sun to extremely high precision, confirming the amount of deflection predicted by general relativity aspect to the 0.03% level.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At this level of precision systematic effects have to be carefully taken into account to determine the precise location of the telescopes on Earth. Some important effects are Earth's
       <a href="/wiki/Nutation" title="Nutation">
        nutation
       </a>
       , rotation, atmospheric refraction, tectonic displacement and tidal waves. Another important effect is refraction of the radio waves by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_corona" title="Solar corona">
        solar corona
       </a>
       . Fortunately, this effect has a characteristic
       <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">
        spectrum
       </a>
       , whereas gravitational distortion is independent of wavelength. Thus, careful analysis, using measurements at several frequencies, can subtract this source of error.
      </p>
      <p>
       The entire sky is slightly distorted due to the gravitational deflection of light caused by the Sun (the anti-Sun direction excepted). This effect has been observed by the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">
        European Space Agency
       </a>
       astrometric satellite
       <a href="/wiki/Hipparcos" title="Hipparcos">
        Hipparcos
       </a>
       . It measured the positions of about 10
       <sup>
        5
       </sup>
       stars. During the full mission about
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7006350000000000000♠
        </span>
        3.5
        <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ×
        </span>
        10
        <sup>
         6
        </sup>
       </span>
       relative positions have been determined, each to an accuracy of typically 3 milliarcseconds (the accuracy for an 8–9 magnitude star). Since the gravitation deflection perpendicular to the Earth–Sun direction is already 4.07 milliarcseconds, corrections are needed for practically all stars. Without systematic effects, the error in an individual observation of 3 milliarcseconds, could be reduced by the square root of the number of positions, leading to a precision of 0.0016 milliarcseconds. Systematic effects, however, limit the accuracy of the determination to 0.3% (Froeschlé, 1997).
      </p>
      <p>
       Launched in 2013, the
       <a href="/wiki/Gaia_(spacecraft)" title="Gaia (spacecraft)">
        <i>
         Gaia
        </i>
        spacecraft
       </a>
       will conduct a census of one billion
       <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
        stars
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">
        Milky Way
       </a>
       and measure their positions to an accuracy of 24 microarcseconds. Thus it will also provide stringent new tests of gravitational deflection of light caused by the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       which was predicted by General relativity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Light_travel_time_delay_testing">
        Light travel time delay testing
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Light travel time delay testing">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Irwin_I._Shapiro" title="Irwin I. Shapiro">
        Irwin I. Shapiro
       </a>
       proposed another test, beyond the classical tests, which could be performed within the Solar System. It is sometimes called the fourth "classical" test of
       <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">
        general relativity
       </a>
       . He predicted a relativistic time delay (
       <a href="/wiki/Shapiro_delay" title="Shapiro delay">
        Shapiro delay
       </a>
       ) in the round-trip travel time for radar signals reflecting off other planets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The mere curvature of the path of a
       <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">
        photon
       </a>
       passing near the Sun is too small to have an observable delaying effect (when the round-trip time is compared to the time taken if the photon had followed a straight path), but general relativity predicts a time delay that becomes progressively larger when the photon passes nearer to the Sun due to the
       <a href="/wiki/Time_dilation" title="Time dilation">
        time dilation
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_potential" title="Gravitational potential">
        gravitational potential
       </a>
       of the Sun. Observing radar reflections from Mercury and Venus just before and after it is eclipsed by the Sun agrees with general relativity theory at the 5% level.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       More recently, the
       <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
        Cassini probe
       </a>
       has undertaken a similar experiment which gave agreement with general relativity at the 0.002% level
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       . However, the following detailed studies
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       revealed that the measured value of the PPN parameter gamma is affected by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitomagnetic" title="Gravitomagnetic">
        gravitomagnetic
       </a>
       effect caused by the orbital motion of Sun around the
       <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">
        barycenter
       </a>
       of the solar system. The gravitomagnetic effect in the
       <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
        Cassini
       </a>
       radioscience experiment was implicitly postulated by B. Berottti as having a pure general relativistic origin but its theoretical value has been never tested in the experiment which effectively makes the experimental uncertainty in the measured value of gamma actually larger (by a factor of 10) than 0.002% claimed by B. Berotti and co-authors in Nature.
      </p>
      <p>
       Very Long Baseline Interferometry has measured velocity-dependent (gravitomagnetic) corrections to the Shapiro time delay in the field of moving Jupiter
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and Saturn.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_equivalence_principle">
        The equivalence principle
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The equivalence principle">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">
        Equivalence principle
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The equivalence principle, in its simplest form, asserts that the trajectories of falling bodies in a gravitational field should be independent of their mass and internal structure, provided they are small enough not to disturb the environment or be affected by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_forces" title="Tidal forces">
        tidal forces
       </a>
       . This idea has been tested to extremely high precision by
       <a href="/wiki/E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s_experiment" title="Eötvös experiment">
        Eötvös torsion balance experiments
       </a>
       , which look for a differential acceleration between two test masses. Constraints on this, and on the existence of a composition-dependent fifth force or gravitational
       <a href="/wiki/Yukawa_interaction" title="Yukawa interaction">
        Yukawa interaction
       </a>
       are very strong, and are discussed under
       <a href="/wiki/Fifth_force" title="Fifth force">
        fifth force
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_equivalence_principle" title="Weak equivalence principle">
        weak equivalence principle
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A version of the equivalence principle, called the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_equivalence_principle" title="Strong equivalence principle">
        strong equivalence principle
       </a>
       , asserts that self-gravitation falling bodies, such as stars, planets or black holes (which are all held together by their gravitational attraction) should follow the same trajectories in a gravitational field, provided the same conditions are satisfied. This is called the
       <a href="/wiki/Nordtvedt_effect" title="Nordtvedt effect">
        Nordtvedt effect
       </a>
       and is most precisely tested by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment" title="Lunar Laser Ranging Experiment">
        Lunar Laser Ranging Experiment
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since 1969, it has continuously measured the distance from several rangefinding stations on Earth to reflectors on the Moon to approximately centimeter accuracy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These have provided a strong constraint on several of the other post-Newtonian parameters.
      </p>
      <p>
       Another part of the strong equivalence principle is the requirement that Newton's gravitational constant be constant in time, and have the same value everywhere in the universe. There are many independent observations limiting the possible variation of Newton's
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">
        gravitational constant
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but one of the best comes from lunar rangefinding which suggests that the gravitational constant does not change by more than one part in 10
       <sup>
        11
       </sup>
       per year. The constancy of the other constants is discussed in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein_equivalence_principle" title="Einstein equivalence principle">
        Einstein equivalence principle
       </a>
       section of the equivalence principle article.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_redshift">
        Gravitational redshift
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Gravitational redshift">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The first of the classical tests discussed above, the
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_redshift" title="Gravitational redshift">
        gravitational redshift
       </a>
       , is a simple consequence of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein_equivalence_principle" title="Einstein equivalence principle">
        Einstein equivalence principle
       </a>
       and was predicted by Einstein in 1907. As such, it is not a test of general relativity in the same way as the post-Newtonian tests, because any theory of gravity obeying the equivalence principle should also incorporate the gravitational redshift. Nonetheless, confirming the existence of the effect was an important substantiation of relativistic gravity, since the absence of gravitational redshift would have strongly contradicted relativity. The first observation of the gravitational redshift was the measurement of the shift in the spectral lines from the
       <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">
        white dwarf
       </a>
       star
       <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">
        Sirius
       </a>
       B by Adams in 1925, discussed above, and follow-on measurements of other white dwarfs. Because of the difficulty of the astrophysical measurement, however, experimental verification using a known terrestrial source was preferable.
      </p>
      <p>
       Experimental verification of gravitational redshift using terrestrial sources took several decades, because it is difficult to find clocks (to measure
       <a href="/wiki/Time_dilation" title="Time dilation">
        time dilation
       </a>
       ) or sources of electromagnetic radiation (to measure redshift) with a frequency that is known well enough that the effect can be accurately measured. It was confirmed experimentally for the first time in 1959 using measurements of the change in wavelength of gamma-ray photons generated with the
       <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ssbauer_effect" title="Mössbauer effect">
        Mössbauer effect
       </a>
       , which generates radiation with a very narrow line width. The
       <a href="/wiki/Pound%E2%80%93Rebka_experiment" title="Pound–Rebka experiment">
        Pound–Rebka experiment
       </a>
       measured the relative redshift of two sources situated at the top and bottom of Harvard University's Jefferson tower.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The result was in excellent agreement with general relativity. This was one of the first precision experiments testing general relativity. The experiment was later improved to better than the 1% level by Pound and Snider.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The blueshift of a falling photon can be found by assuming it has an equivalent mass based on its frequency
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle E=hf}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              E
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mi>
              h
             </mi>
             <mi>
              f
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle E=hf}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="E=hf " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f39fac3593bb1e2dec0282c112c4dff7a99007f6" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.492ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
       (where
       <i>
        h
       </i>
       is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_constant" title="Planck's constant">
        Planck's constant
       </a>
       ) along with
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              E
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mi>
              m
             </mi>
             <msup>
              <mi>
               c
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mn>
                2
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msup>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle E=mc^{2}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="E=mc^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9f73dbd37a0cac34406ee89057fa1b36a1e6a18e" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.976ex; height:2.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       , a result of special relativity. Such simple derivations ignore the fact that in general relativity the experiment compares clock rates, rather than energies. In other words, the "higher energy" of the photon after it falls can be equivalently ascribed to the slower running of clocks deeper in the gravitational potential well. To fully validate general relativity, it is important to also show that the rate of arrival of the photons is greater than the rate at which they are emitted. A very accurate gravitational redshift experiment, which deals with this issue, was performed in 1976,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       where a
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
        hydrogen
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Maser" title="Maser">
        maser
       </a>
       clock on a rocket was launched to a height of 10,000 km, and its rate compared with an identical clock on the ground. It tested the gravitational redshift to 0.007%.
      </p>
      <p>
       Although the
       <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">
        Global Positioning System
       </a>
       (GPS) is not designed as a test of fundamental physics, it must account for the gravitational redshift in its timing system, and physicists have analyzed timing data from the GPS to confirm other tests. When the first satellite was launched, some engineers resisted the prediction that a noticeable gravitational time dilation would occur, so the first satellite was launched without the clock adjustment that was later built into subsequent satellites. It showed the predicted shift of 38 microseconds per day. This rate of discrepancy is sufficient to substantially impair function of GPS within hours if not accounted for. An excellent account of the role played by general relativity in the design of GPS can be found in Ashby 2003.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Other precision tests of general relativity,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       not discussed here, are the
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Probe_A" title="Gravity Probe A">
        Gravity Probe A
       </a>
       satellite, launched in 1976, which showed gravity and velocity affect the ability to synchronize the rates of clocks orbiting a central mass; the
       <a href="/wiki/Hafele%E2%80%93Keating_experiment" title="Hafele–Keating experiment">
        Hafele–Keating experiment
       </a>
       , which used atomic clocks in circumnavigating aircraft to test general relativity and special relativity together;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the forthcoming
       <a href="/wiki/STEP_(satellite)" title="STEP (satellite)">
        Satellite Test of the Equivalence Principle
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Frame-dragging_tests">
        Frame-dragging tests
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Frame-dragging tests">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LAGEOS-NASA.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="784" data-file-width="784" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/LAGEOS-NASA.jpg/150px-LAGEOS-NASA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/LAGEOS-NASA.jpg/225px-LAGEOS-NASA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/LAGEOS-NASA.jpg/300px-LAGEOS-NASA.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LAGEOS-NASA.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The LAGEOS-1 satellite. (
         <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">
          D
         </a>
         =60 cm)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Frame-dragging" title="Frame-dragging">
        Frame-dragging
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Tests of the
       <a href="/wiki/Lense%E2%80%93Thirring_precession" title="Lense–Thirring precession">
        Lense–Thirring precession
       </a>
       , consisting of small secular
       <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">
        precessions
       </a>
       of the orbit of a test particle in motion around a central rotating mass, for example, a planet or a star, have been performed with the
       <a href="/wiki/LAGEOS" title="LAGEOS">
        LAGEOS
       </a>
       satellites,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but many aspects of them remain controversial. The same effect may have been detected in the data of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">
        Mars Global Surveyor
       </a>
       (MGS) spacecraft, a former probe in orbit around
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       ; also such a test raised a debate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       First attempts to detect the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       's Lense–Thirring effect on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelia" title="Perihelia">
        perihelia
       </a>
       of the inner
       <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">
        planets
       </a>
       have been recently reported as well. Frame dragging would cause the orbital plane of stars orbiting near a
       <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">
        supermassive black hole
       </a>
       to precess about the black hole spin axis. This effect should be detectable within the next few years via
       <a href="/wiki/Astrometry" title="Astrometry">
        astrometric
       </a>
       monitoring of stars at the center of the
       <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">
        Milky Way
       </a>
       galaxy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By comparing the rate of orbital precession of two stars on different orbits, it is possible in principle to test the
       <a href="/wiki/No-hair_theorem" title="No-hair theorem">
        no-hair theorems
       </a>
       of general relativity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Probe_B" title="Gravity Probe B">
        Gravity Probe B
       </a>
       satellite, launched in 2004 and operated until 2005, detected frame-dragging and the
       <a href="/wiki/Geodetic_effect" title="Geodetic effect">
        geodetic effect
       </a>
       . The experiment used four quartz spheres the size of ping pong balls coated with a superconductor. Data analysis continued through 2011 due to high noise levels and difficulties in modelling the noise accurately so that a useful signal could be found. Principal investigators at
       <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">
        Stanford University
       </a>
       reported on May 4, 2011, that they had accurately measured the frame dragging effect relative to the distant star
       <a href="/wiki/IM_Pegasi" title="IM Pegasi">
        IM Pegasi
       </a>
       , and the calculations proved to be in line with the prediction of Einstein's theory. The results, published in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Physical_Review_Letters" title="Physical Review Letters">
         Physical Review Letters
        </a>
       </i>
       measured the
       <a href="/wiki/Geodetic_effect" title="Geodetic effect">
        geodetic effect
       </a>
       with an error of about 0.2 percent. The results reported the frame dragging effect (caused by Earth's rotation) added up to 37 milliarcseconds with an error of about 19 percent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Everitt_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Everitt-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Investigator Francis Everitt explained that a milliarcsecond "is the width of a human hair seen at the distance of 10 miles".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In January 2012,
       <a href="/wiki/LARES_(satellite)" title="LARES (satellite)">
        LARES
       </a>
       satellite was launched on a
       <a href="/wiki/Vega_(rocket)" title="Vega (rocket)">
        Vega
       </a>
       rocket
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to measure
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lense%E2%80%93Thirring_effect" title="Lense–Thirring effect">
        Lense–Thirring effect
       </a>
       with an accuracy of about 1%, according to its proponents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This evaluation of the actual accuracy obtainable is a subject of debate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ciufolini2009_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ciufolini2009-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tests_of_the_gravitational_potential_at_small_distances">
        Tests of the gravitational potential at small distances
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Tests of the gravitational potential at small distances">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       It is possible to test whether the gravitational potential continues with the inverse square law at very small distances. Tests so far have focused on a divergence from GR in the form of a
       <a href="/wiki/Yukawa_potential" title="Yukawa potential">
        Yukawa potential
       </a>
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle V(r)=V_{0}(1+\alpha e^{-r/\lambda })}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              V
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              (
             </mo>
             <mi>
              r
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              )
             </mo>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               V
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mn>
                0
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              (
             </mo>
             <mn>
              1
             </mn>
             <mo>
              +
             </mo>
             <mi>
              α
              <!-- α -->
             </mi>
             <msup>
              <mi>
               e
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mo>
                −
                <!-- − -->
               </mo>
               <mi>
                r
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mo>
                 /
                </mo>
               </mrow>
               <mi>
                λ
                <!-- λ -->
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msup>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              )
             </mo>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle V(r)=V_{0}(1+\alpha e^{-r/\lambda })}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\displaystyle V(r)=V_{0}(1+\alpha e^{-r/\lambda })}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6051d5fefb5467ee9311b351cd5144502a55cf5a" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:22.569ex; height:3.343ex;"/>
       </span>
       , but no evidence for a potential of this kind has been found. The Yukawa potential with
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \alpha =1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
