          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              α
              <!-- α -->
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              1
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \alpha =1}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\alpha =1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/03d67a45a44be8b8f15e99b7def2b0cf0aba1717" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.749ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       has been ruled out down to
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \lambda =5.6\times 10^{-5}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              λ
              <!-- λ -->
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              5.6
             </mn>
             <mo>
              ×
              <!-- × -->
             </mo>
             <msup>
              <mn>
               10
              </mn>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mo>
                −
                <!-- − -->
               </mo>
               <mn>
                5
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msup>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \lambda =5.6\times 10^{-5}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\displaystyle \lambda =5.6\times 10^{-5}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/46cc6db988208612d2b976d9f6b1fcd17506a296" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:14.924ex; height:2.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       m.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Strong_field_tests">
        Strong field tests
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Strong field tests">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The very strong gravitational fields that are present close to
       <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">
        black holes
       </a>
       , especially those
       <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">
        supermassive black holes
       </a>
       which are thought to power
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_galactic_nuclei" title="Active galactic nuclei">
        active galactic nuclei
       </a>
       and the more active
       <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">
        quasars
       </a>
       , belong to a field of intense active research. Observations of these quasars and active galactic nuclei are difficult, and interpretation of the observations is heavily dependent upon astrophysical models other than general relativity or competing fundamental
       <a href="/wiki/Alternatives_to_general_relativity" title="Alternatives to general relativity">
        theories of gravitation
       </a>
       , but they are qualitatively consistent with the black hole concept as modelled in general relativity.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Binary_pulsars">
        Binary pulsars
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Binary pulsars">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_pulsar" title="Binary pulsar">
        Binary pulsar
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_pulsar_PSR_J0348%2B0432_and_its_white_dwarf_companion.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3125" data-file-width="5000" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_pulsar_PSR_J0348%2B0432_and_its_white_dwarf_companion.jpg/300px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_pulsar_PSR_J0348%2B0432_and_its_white_dwarf_companion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_pulsar_PSR_J0348%2B0432_and_its_white_dwarf_companion.jpg/450px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_pulsar_PSR_J0348%2B0432_and_its_white_dwarf_companion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_pulsar_PSR_J0348%2B0432_and_its_white_dwarf_companion.jpg/600px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_pulsar_PSR_J0348%2B0432_and_its_white_dwarf_companion.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_the_pulsar_PSR_J0348%2B0432_and_its_white_dwarf_companion.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artist's impression of the pulsar
         <a href="/wiki/PSR_J0348%2B0432" title="PSR J0348+0432">
          PSR J0348+0432
         </a>
         and its white dwarf companion radiating
         <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">
          gravitational waves
         </a>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
          <a href="#cite_note-72">
           [72]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulsars" title="Pulsars">
        Pulsars
       </a>
       are rapidly rotating
       <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">
        neutron stars
       </a>
       which emit regular radio pulses as they rotate. As such they act as clocks which allow very precise monitoring of their orbital motions. Observations of pulsars in orbit around other stars have all demonstrated substantial
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periapsis" title="Periapsis">
        periapsis
       </a>
       precessions that cannot be accounted for classically but can be accounted for by using general relativity. For example, the Hulse–Taylor
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_pulsar" title="Binary pulsar">
        binary pulsar
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PSR_B1913%2B16" title="PSR B1913+16">
        PSR B1913+16
       </a>
       (a pair of neutron stars in which one is detected as a pulsar) has an observed precession of over 4° of arc per year (periastron shift per orbit only about 10
       <sup>
        −6
       </sup>
       ). This precession has been used to compute the masses of the components.
      </p>
      <p>
       Similarly to the way in which atoms and molecules emit electromagnetic radiation, a gravitating mass that is in
       <a href="/wiki/Quadrupole" title="Quadrupole">
        quadrupole
       </a>
       type or higher order vibration, or is asymmetric and in rotation, can emit gravitational waves.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_waves" title="Gravitational waves">
        gravitational waves
       </a>
       are predicted to travel at the
       <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">
        speed of light
       </a>
       . For example, planets orbiting the Sun constantly lose energy via gravitational radiation, but this effect is so small that it is unlikely it will be observed in the near future (Earth radiates about 200 watts (see
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">
        gravitational waves
       </a>
       ) of gravitational radiation).
      </p>
      <p>
       The radiation of gravitational waves has been inferred from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PSR_B1913%2B16" title="PSR B1913+16">
        Hulse–Taylor binary
       </a>
       (and other binary pulsars).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Precise timing of the pulses shows that the stars orbit only approximately according to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_Laws" title="Kepler's Laws">
        Kepler's Laws
       </a>
       : over time they gradually spiral towards each other, demonstrating an
       <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
        energy
       </a>
       loss in close agreement with the predicted energy radiated by gravitational waves.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For their discovery of the first binary pulsar and measuring its orbital decay due to gravitational-wave emission,
       <a href="/wiki/Russell_Alan_Hulse" title="Russell Alan Hulse">
        Hulse
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Hooton_Taylor,_Jr." title="Joseph Hooton Taylor, Jr.">
        Taylor
       </a>
       won the 1993
       <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">
        Nobel Prize in Physics
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A "double pulsar" discovered in 2003,
       <a href="/wiki/PSR_J0737-3039" title="PSR J0737-3039">
        PSR J0737-3039
       </a>
       , has a periastron precession of 16.90° per year; unlike the Hulse–Taylor binary, both
       <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">
        neutron stars
       </a>
       are detected as pulsars, allowing precision timing of both members of the system. Due to this, the tight orbit, the fact that the system is almost edge-on, and the very low transverse velocity of the system as seen from Earth, J0737−3039 provides by far the best system for strong-field tests of general relativity known so far. Several distinct relativistic effects are observed, including orbital decay as in the Hulse–Taylor system. After observing the system for two and a half years, four independent tests of general relativity were possible, the most precise (the Shapiro delay) confirming the general relativity prediction within 0.05%
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (nevertheless the periastron shift per orbit is only about 0.0013% of a circle and thus it is not a higher-order relativity test).
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2013, an international team of astronomers reported new data from observing a pulsar-white dwarf system
       <a href="/wiki/PSR_J0348%2B0432" title="PSR J0348+0432">
        PSR J0348+0432
       </a>
       , in which they have been able to measure a change in the orbital period of 8 millionths of a second per year, and confirmed GR predictions in a regime of extreme gravitational fields never probed before;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but there are still some competing theories that would agree with these data.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_detection_of_gravitational_waves">
        Direct detection of gravitational waves
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Direct detection of gravitational waves">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A number of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational-wave_detector" title="Gravitational-wave detector">
        gravitational-wave detectors
       </a>
       have been built with the intent of directly detecting the gravitational waves emanating from such astronomical events as the merger of two
       <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">
        neutron stars
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">
        black holes
       </a>
       . In February 2016, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_LIGO" title="Advanced LIGO">
        Advanced LIGO
       </a>
       team announced that they had
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave_detection" title="Gravitational wave detection">
        directly detected gravitational waves
       </a>
       from a stellar
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_black_hole" title="Binary black hole">
        binary black hole
       </a>
       merger,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery_2016_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Discovery_2016-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSF_82-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NSF-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with additional detections announced in June 2016, June 2017, and August 2017.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conover2017_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-conover2017-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       General relativity predicts gravitational waves, as does any theory of gravitation in which changes in the gravitational field propagate at a finite speed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since gravitational waves can be directly detected,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery_2016_1-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Discovery_2016-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSF_82-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NSF-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it is possible to use them to learn about the Universe. This is
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational-wave_astronomy" title="Gravitational-wave astronomy">
        gravitational-wave astronomy
       </a>
       . Gravitational-wave astronomy can test general relativity by verifying that the observed waves are of the form predicted (for example, that they only have two transverse polarizations), and by checking that
       <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">
        black holes
       </a>
       are the objects described by solutions of the
       <a href="/wiki/Einstein_field_equations" title="Einstein field equations">
        Einstein field equations
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tests_87-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tests-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       "These amazing observations are the confirmation of a lot of theoretical work, including Einstein's general theory of relativity, which predicts gravitational waves," says physicist Stephen Hawking.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery_2016_1-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Discovery_2016-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmological_tests">
        Cosmological tests
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cosmological tests">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Tests of general relativity on the largest scales are not nearly so stringent as Solar System tests.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The earliest such test was prediction and discovery of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expansion_of_the_universe" title="Expansion of the universe">
        expansion of the universe
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudnicki28_89-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rudnicki28-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1922,
       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Friedmann" title="Alexander Friedmann">
        Alexander Friedmann
       </a>
       found that Einstein equations have non-stationary solutions (even in the presence of the
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">
        cosmological constant
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pauli1_90-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pauli1-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1927,
       <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lemaître">
        Georges Lemaître
       </a>
       showed that static solutions of the Einstein equations, which are possible in the presence of the cosmological constant, are unstable, and therefore the static universe envisioned by Einstein could not exist (it must either expand or contract).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pauli1_90-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pauli1-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Lemaître made an explicit prediction that the universe should expand.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kragh,_2003,_p._153_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kragh,_2003,_p._153-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He also derived a redshift-distance relationship, which is now known as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Law" title="Hubble Law">
        Hubble Law
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kragh,_2003,_p._153_92-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kragh,_2003,_p._153-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Later, in 1931, Einstein himself agreed with the results of Friedmann and Lemaître.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pauli1_90-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pauli1-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The expansion of the universe discovered by
       <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">
        Edwin Hubble
       </a>
       in 1929
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pauli1_90-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pauli1-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       was then considered by many (and continues to be considered by some now) as a direct confirmation of general relativity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 1930s, largely due to the work of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._A._Milne" title="E. A. Milne">
        E. A. Milne
       </a>
       , it was realised that the linear relationship between redshift and distance derives from the general assumption of uniformity and isotropy rather than specifically from general relativity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudnicki28_89-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rudnicki28-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However the prediction of a non-static universe was non-trivial, indeed dramatic, and primarily motivated by general relativity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some other cosmological tests include searches for primordial gravitational waves generated during
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" title="Cosmic inflation">
        cosmic inflation
       </a>
       , which may be detected in the
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">
        cosmic microwave background
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">
        polarization
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or by a proposed space-based
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational-wave_detector" title="Gravitational-wave detector">
        gravitational-wave interferometer
       </a>
       called the
       <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_Observer" title="Big Bang Observer">
        Big Bang Observer
       </a>
       . Other tests at high redshift are constraints on other theories of gravity,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the variation of the gravitational constant since
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big bang nucleosynthesis">
        big bang nucleosynthesis
       </a>
       (it varied by no more than 40% since then).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In August 2017, the findings of tests conducted by astronomers using the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Southern_Observatory" title="European Southern Observatory">
        European Southern Observatory
       </a>
       ’s
       <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">
        Very Large Telescope
       </a>
       (VLT), among other instruments, were released, and which positively demonstrated gravitational effects predicted by Albert Einstein. One of which tests observed the orbit of the stars circling around
       <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_A*" title="Sagittarius A*">
        Sagittarius A*
       </a>
       , a black hole about 4 million times as massive as the sun. Einstein’s theory suggested that large objects bend the space around them, causing other objects to diverge from the straight lines they would otherwise follow. Although previous studies have validated Einstein's theory, this was the first time his theory had been tested on such a gigantic object. The findings were published in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Astrophysical_Journal" title="The Astrophysical Journal">
         The Astrophysical Journal
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein-TheoryOfRelativity_98-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Einstein-TheoryOfRelativity-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SeanDuffy2017_99-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SeanDuffy2017-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>

         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          In general relativity, a perfectly spherical star (in vacuum) that expands or contracts while remaining perfectly spherical
          <i>
           cannot
          </i>
          emit any gravitational waves (similar to the lack of e/m radiation from a pulsating charge), as
          <a href="/wiki/Birkhoff%27s_theorem_(relativity)" title="Birkhoff's theorem (relativity)">
           Birkhoff's theorem
          </a>
          says that the geometry remains the same exterior to the star. More generally, a rotating system will only emit gravitational waves if it lacks the axial symmetry with respect to the axis of rotation.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-74">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=History+of+Science&amp;rft.atitle=Who+discovered+the+expanding+universe&amp;rft.volume=41&amp;rft.pages=141-62&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1177%2F007327530304100202&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2003HisSc..41..141K&amp;rft.aulast=Kragh&amp;rft.aufirst=Helge&amp;rft.au=Smith%2C+Robert+W.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fadsabs.harvard.edu%2Ffull%2F2003HisSc..41..141K&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATests+of+general+relativity">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
       </ul>
       <h3>
        <span class="mw-headline" id="Textbooks">
         Textbooks
        </span>
        <span class="mw-editsection">
         <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
          [
         </span>
         <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Textbooks">
          edit
         </a>
         <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
          ]
         </span>
        </span>
       </h3>
       <ul>
        <li>
         S. M. Carroll,
         <i>
          <a class="external text" href="http://pancake.uchicago.edu/~carroll/grbook/" rel="nofollow">
           Spacetime and Geometry: an Introduction to General Relativity
          </a>
          <sup class="noprint Inline-Template">
           <span style="white-space: nowrap;">
            [
            <i>
             <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot">
              <span title=" Dead link since January 2018">
               permanent dead link
              </span>
             </a>
            </i>
            ]
           </span>
          </sup>
         </i>
         , Addison-Wesley, 2003. An introductory general relativity textbook.
        </li>
        <li>
         A. S. Eddington,
         <i>
          <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=7_U48JyJneQC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;source=gbs_ge_summary_r&amp;cad=0#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">
           Space, Time and Gravitation
          </a>
         </i>
         , Cambridge University Press, reprint of 1920 ed.
        </li>
        <li>
         A. Gefter, "Putting Einstein to the Test",
         <i>
          Sky and Telescope
         </i>
         July 2005, p. 38. A popular discussion of tests of general relativity.
        </li>
        <li>
         H. Ohanian and R. Ruffini,
         <i>
          Gravitation and Spacetime, 2nd Edition
         </i>
         Norton, New York, 1994,
         <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
          ISBN
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-393-96501-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-393-96501-5">
          0-393-96501-5
         </a>
         . A general relativity textbook.
        </li>
        <li>
         <span id="Pauli">
          <cite class="citation book">
           Pauli, Wolfgang Ernst (1958). "Part IV. General Theory of Relativity".
           <i>
            Theory of Relativity
           </i>
           . Courier Dover Publications.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-486-64152-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-486-64152-2">
            978-0-486-64152-2
           </a>
           .
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Part+IV.+General+Theory+of+Relativity&amp;rft.btitle=Theory+of+Relativity&amp;rft.pub=Courier+Dover+Publications&amp;rft.date=1958&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-486-64152-2&amp;rft.aulast=Pauli&amp;rft.aufirst=Wolfgang+Ernst&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATests+of+general+relativity">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li>
         C. M. Will,
         <i>
          Theory and Experiment in Gravitational Physics
         </i>
         , Cambridge University Press, Cambridge (1993). A standard technical reference.
        </li>
        <li>
         C. M. Will,
         <i>
          Was Einstein Right?: Putting General Relativity to the Test
         </i>
         , Basic Books (1993). This is a popular account of tests of general relativity.
        </li>
       </ul>
       <h3>
        <span class="mw-headline" id="Living_Reviews_papers">
         Living Reviews papers
        </span>
        <span class="mw-editsection">
         <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
          [
         </span>
         <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tests_of_general_relativity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Living Reviews papers">
          edit
         </a>
         <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
          ]
         </span>
        </span>
       </h3>
       <ul>
        <li>
         N. Ashby,
         <a class="external text" href="https://link.springer.com/article/10.12942/lrr-2003-1" rel="nofollow">
          "Relativity in the Global Positioning System"
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          Living Reviews in Relativity
         </i>
         (2003).
        </li>
        <li>
         C. M. Will,
         <a class="external text" href="https://link.springer.com/article/10.12942/lrr-2014-4" rel="nofollow">
          The Confrontation between General Relativity and Experiment
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          Living Reviews in Relativity
         </i>
         (2014). An online, technical review, covering much of the material in
         <i>
          Theory and experiment in gravitational physics.
         </i>
         It is less comprehensive but more up to date. (ArXiv version here:
         <a class="external text" href="https://arxiv.org/abs/1403.7377" rel="nofollow">
          arxiv.org/abs/1403.7377
         </a>
         )
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
